The Principle of Mahdhoof Not Like Mazkoor: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
States the large disputed principle and responds to a Shafi'i objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the omitted (mahdhoof) is not treated like the mentioned (mazkoor) to objections about generality in muqtada.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, an omitted word does not acquire properties of explicit wording; generality comes only from the explicit text.

This lesson examines a foundational dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over the principle of 'al-mahdhoof ka al-mazkoor' (the omitted is like the mentioned). The Hanafis reject this principle, maintaining that omitted elements (muqtada) do not carry rulings such as generality. An objection from a Shafi'i perspective uses the phrase 'a'tiq 'abdaka 'anni' to argue that Hanafis inconsistently assume generality in a muqtada. We will follow the Hanafi response, which locates the generality in the explicit word 'your slave' rather than the implied sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed principle
State the principle 'al-mahdhoof ka al-mazkoor' and its meaning: the omitted is like the mentioned.
This principle is accepted by Shafi'is and rejected by Hanafis.
2
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis hold that the omitted (mahdhoof) is not like the mentioned (mazkoor); thus, muqtada does not inherit properties like generality.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
In 'a'tiq 'abdaka 'anni', an omitted sale is required; Hanafis say it includes all slaves, appearing to grant generality to muqtada.
The objection claims Hanafis contradict themselves.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The phrase is unpacked to include explicit mention: 'Sell your slave for me, then be my agent in freeing him.' The generality stems from the explicit word 'abdaka', not from the omitted sale.
Thus, no contradiction.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in any case with an omission, Hanafis restrict the ruling to explicit wording; Shafi'is extend it to the omitted element.
This affects many fiqh rulings.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: هذا اصل كبير مختلف الخ ماتن فرماتے ہیں کہ المحذوف کالمذكور شوافع کے ہاں ایک مسلمہ اصول ہے جس کے ہم منکر ہیں۔ اسی اصل کبیر پر ہمارے اور انکے درمیان مختلف مسائل میں اختلافات ہوئے ہیں۔ قوله ولا يقال ان قوله اعتق علیک عنی :. عبارت احناف کے مذھب پر وارد ہونے والے ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے اعتراض کی تقریر :۔ ۔ آپ ایک طرف تو کہتے ہیں کہ مقتضی میں عموم نہیں ہوتا جبکہ اس مثال میں آپ نے خود مقتضی میں عموم مان کر اپنے مذھب سے انحراف کیا ہے ۔ مثال یہ ہے کہ قائل نے کہا اعتق عبدك عنی اس میں اقتضاء کے طور پر بیع ماننا ضروری ہے، لہذا اسمیں عموم نہیں ہونا چاہئے حالانکہ آپ بھی کہتے ہیں کہ اس کا قول تمام غلاموں کی بیع کو شامل ہے جواب:۔۔۔ آپ کو غلط نہیں ہوئی ہے ہم اس مسئلے میں جو تمام غلاموں کی بیع کا قول کرتے ہیں وہ مقتضی میں عموم ماننے کی وجہ سے نہیں کرتے بلکہ یہ کلام اس کلام کے معنی میں ہے: بع عبدك عنى ثم كن وكیلی بالاعتاق اور یہاں عبدک کا لفظ عبارت میں صراحتا مذکور ہے اس کے عموم کی وجہ سے ہم کہتے ہیں کہ یہ قول تمام غلاموں کی بیع کو شامل ہے، لہذا آپکی یہ بات بے بنیاد ہے کہ ہم نے مقتضی کو عام مانا ہے۔
His statement: 'This is a great disputed principle...' The author says: 'al-mahdhoof ka al-mazkoor (the omitted is like the mentioned)' is an accepted principle among the Shafi'is, which we deny. On this great principle, many differences have arisen between us and them. His statement: 'And it is not said that his statement 'a'tiq 'alayka 'anni'...' This phrase is the response to an objection raised against the Hanafi school. Presentation of the objection: On one hand you say that there is no generality in muqtada, but in this example you have yourself admitted generality in muqtada, thereby deviating from your school. The example is: The speaker said 'a'tiq 'abdaka 'anni' (free your slave on my behalf). In this, it is necessary to assume a sale by way of iqtidaa. Therefore, there should be no generality in it, whereas you also say that his statement includes the sale of all slaves. Response: You have not made a mistake; what we say about the sale of all slaves in this issue is not because we accept generality in muqtada, but rather this speech is in the meaning of: 'Sell your slave for me, then be my agent in freeing him.' And here the word 'your slave' ('abdaka) is explicitly mentioned in the statement, and due to its generality, we say that this statement includes the sale of all slaves. Therefore, your claim that we have considered muqtada as general is baseless.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul principle: 'al-mahdhoof ka al-mazkoor' (the omitted is like the mentioned). The Shafi'is accept this principle, meaning that when a word is omitted from speech but required by context, it is treated as if it were uttered, and thus it can carry generality. The Hanafis reject this principle. An objection is raised: In the phrase 'a'tiq 'abdaka 'anni' (free your slave for me), the Hanafis say that iqtidaa requires assuming a sale, yet they also say that the phrase includes all slaves (implying generality). How is that consistent? The Hanafi response: The generality comes not from the muqtada but from the explicitly mentioned word 'your slave' ('abdaka). The full implied meaning is 'Sell your slave for me, then be my agent in freeing him.' The word 'your slave' is explicit and carries generality. Thus, the Hanafi position remains consistent: muqtada itself has no generality; the generality is from the explicit wording.

Key Terms
محذوف (mahdhoof): An omitted word or phrase that must be assumed to complete the meaning of the text.
مذکور (mazkoor): The explicit word that is actually mentioned in the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafis, why does 'a'tiq 'abdaka 'anni' include all of the speaker's slaves?
A) Because muqtada (the implied sale) is general
B) Because the word 'abdaka is explicit and general
C) Because the Shafi'i principle is accepted here
D) Because the context forces generality

Key Takeaway
Hanafis reject the principle that omitted words are like mentioned ones; rulings are derived from explicit texts only.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
10 min
The Generality of Muqtada: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains the usul dispute and applies it to an oath case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on muqtada and apply it to the validity of an oath.

Core Idea
Muqtada, as a meaning, lacks generality; only words have umūm.

At the heart of a classic usul dispute lies the question: does the implied element (muqtada) in a statement carry generality (umum) or specificity (khusus)? The Hanafis argue that muqtada belongs to the realm of meaning, not words, and thus cannot be general. The Shafi'is treat muqtada like an omitted word (mahzuf), which can have general scope. This lesson will walk you through the proofs and apply the principle to a real fiqh scenario.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi claim that muqtada does not have umum or khusus because it is a meaning, not a word.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and their proof
Recognize the Shafi'i view that muqtada is analogous to mahzuf which is considered a word, so it can have generality.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to an objection
Study why the example 'Set free your slave on my behalf' does not contradict the Hanafi principle – the generality comes from the explicit word 'your slave', not from the muqtada.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: in an oath like 'If I eat, my slave is free,' the muqtada 'food' has no generality, so any food breaks the oath, and intention cannot restrict it.
5
Apply the Hanafi rule to the example
Work through the oath scenario step-by-step, confirming that the oath-breaker is liable for any food regardless of his intention.
Hanafis hold that muqtada cannot be particularized, so the oath-breaker is bound by every kind of food.

احناف کی دلیل : ۔ ۔ ۔ عموم اور خصوص الفاظ کے عوارض میں سے ہیں جبکہ مقتضی معنی کی قبیل سے ہے۔
(۲) شوافع کا مذھب ۔ ۔ ۔ شوافع اہل اصول کے ہاں مقتضی میں عموم ہے
شوافع کی دلیل :۔۔ شوافع مقتضی کو محذوف کی طرح قرار دیتے ہیں جو ملفوظ ہوتا ہے لہذا مقتضی بھی الفاظ کی قبیل سے ہوگا ۔ اور اسمیں عموم و خصوص جاری ہو نگے ۔
قوله: هذا اصل كبير مختلف الخ ماتن فرماتے ہیں کہ المحذوف کالمذكور شوافع کے ہاں ایک مسلمہ اصول ہے جس کے ہم منکر ہیں۔ اسی اصل کبیر پر ہمارے اور انکے درمیان مختلف مسائل میں اختلافات ہوئے ہیں۔

جواب:۔۔۔ آپ کو غلط نہیں ہوئی ہے ہم اس مسئلے میں جو تمام غلاموں کی بیع کا قول کرتے ہیں وہ مقتضی میں عموم ماننے کی وجہ سے نہیں کرتے بلکہ یہ کلام اس کلام کے معنی میں ہے: بع عبدك عنى ثم كن وكیلی بالاعتاق اور یہاں عبدک کا لفظ عبارت میں صراحتا مذکور ہے اس کے عموم کی وجہ سے ہم کہتے ہیں کہ یہ قول تمام غلاموں کی بیع کو شامل ہے، لہذا آپکی یہ بات بے بنیاد ہے کہ ہم نے مقتضی کو عام مانا ہے۔
مقتضی میں عموم اور عدم عموم پر مختلف مسائل کی تفریح
قوله حتى اذا قال ان اكلت فعبدی حر ونوى الخ آپ ماقبل میں یہ بات دلائل کی روشنی میں سمجھ چکے ہیں کہ احناف کے ہاں مقتضی میں عموم و خصوص جاری نہیں ہوتے جبکہ حضرت شوافع اس کے قائل ہیں۔ اب صاحب کتاب فقہی مسائل کی روشنی میں بطور تفریح یہی اصول واضح فرما رہے ہیں۔
(1) پہلی تفریح صورت مسئلہ فقیہ:۔۔۔ کسی شخص نے کہا ان اكلت فعبدی حر ظاہری بات ہے کہ اس کا کلام تبھی درست ہو گا جب بطور اقتضاء لفظ طعاماً مقدر مانا جائے ۔ اب اگر یہ کہے کہ اس سے میں نے خاص قسم کے کھانے کی نیت کی ہے مثلاً ان اكلت اللحم فعبدی حر ، دوسرے کھانے مراد نہیں تو ایسے شخص کی تصدیق کی جائے گی یا نہیں۔ قضاء تو بالاتفاق اس کی نیت کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ یہ خلاف ظاہر کی نیت ہے جبکہ قاضی (شرع) ظاہر کا مکلف ہے۔
احناف کے ہاں دیانةً بھی اس کی نیت معتبر نہیں :
احناف کہتے ہیں کہ دیانةً اس لئے اسکی نیت صحیح نہیں ہے کہ اس کے کلام میں لفظ طعاماً مذکور نہیں بلکہ یہ اکل کے اقتضاء سے مستفاد ہے کیونکہ اکل بدون طعام ممکن نہیں اور مقتضی میں عموم نہیں ہوتا نہ خصوص ۔ اس لئے یہ جس قسم کا بھی کھانا کھانے حانث تصور ہوگا۔ خواہ نیت طعام دون طعام کی کیوں نہ کرے وجہ یہ ہے کہ جب طعام مقتضی میں عموم نہیں تو یہ قابل تخصیص بھی نہیں ہوگا ۔
وأما حنثه بكل طعام الخ.
صاحب نور الانور شیخ احمد رحمہ اللہ ایک اعتراض کو دور کر رہے ہیں:
اعتراض :- - جب آپ کہتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں طعام میں عموم نہیں تو ہر قسم کا کھانا کھانے سے قائل کو حانث نہیں ہونا چاہئے ۔
The proof of the Hanafis: Generality and particularity are among the accidents of words, while muqtada belongs to the category of meaning. (2) The madhhab of the Shafi'is: According to the scholars of usul, muqtada has generality. The proof of the Shafi'is: They treat muqtada like the omitted word (mahzuf) which is actually uttered, so muqtada will also belong to the category of words, and generality and particularity may apply to it. Qawlahu: 'This is a great principle regarding which there is difference' etc. The author says that 'the omitted is like the mentioned' is an established principle among the Shafi'is, which we deny. Differences have arisen between us and them in various issues on the basis of this great principle. Qawlahu: 'And it should not be said that his statement 'Set free your slave on my behalf' ...' This expression is a response to an objection raised against the Hanafi madhhab. Explanation of the objection: On one hand you say that muqtada does not have generality, but in this example you yourselves have deviated from your madhhab by assuming generality in muqtada. The example is that the speaker said 'Set free your slave on my behalf.' In this, it is necessary to assume a sale as a required element. So there should be no generality in it, yet you also say that his statement includes the sale of all his slaves. The answer: You are not mistaken. In this issue, we do not say that it includes the sale of all slaves because we assume generality in muqtada. Rather, this statement is equivalent to: 'Sell your slave on my behalf, then be my agent for manumission.' And the word 'your slave' (عبدک) is explicitly mentioned in the expression, so due to its generality we say that this statement includes the sale of all his slaves. Therefore, your assertion that we have assumed generality in muqtada is baseless. The derivation of different issues on generality and non-generality in muqtada. Qawlahu: 'Until when he says: if I eat, my slave is free, and he intended' etc. You have already understood from the proofs above that according to the Hanafis, generality and particularity do not apply to muqtada, while the Shafi'is affirm them. Now the author is clarifying this principle by way of derivation in the light of fiqh issues. (1) The first derivation: The case of the jurist: A person said: 'If I eat, my slave is free.' It is obvious that his speech will only be valid if the word 'food' (طعام) is assumed as an implied required element. Now if he says that he intended a specific type of food, e.g., 'If I eat meat, my slave is free,' intending no other food, will such a person's intention be accepted in court? In the judiciary (qada'), by consensus, his intention will not be considered because it is an intention contrary to the apparent meaning, and the judge (shar') is obligated [to judge] by the apparent. According to the Hanafis, even in private (diyanatan), his intention is not valid. The Hanafis say that in private it is not valid because the word 'food' is not mentioned in his speech; rather, it is derived from the required element of eating, since eating without food is impossible, and muqtada has neither generality nor particularity. Therefore, he will be considered an oath-breaker regardless of what type of food he eats. Even if he intends food without specifying, the reason is that when the required element (muqtada) has no generality, it cannot be particularized. As for his breaking the oath by every food... The author of Nur al-Anwar, Shaykh Ahmad, may Allah have mercy on him, is removing an objection: Objection: When you say that in this case there is no generality in 'food,' then the oath-breaker should not be considered liable for eating every type of food.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the legal status of al-muqtada (the required or implied element). The Hanafis argue that generality (al-umum) and specificity (al-khusus) are attributes of words (alfaz), whereas muqtada belongs to the realm of meaning (al-ma'na), which is not subject to these attributes. Therefore, muqtada does not carry generality. The Shafi'is, on the other hand, analogize muqtada to the omitted word (al-mahzuf) which, though omitted, is considered like a spoken word in legal effect. According to them, since the omitted word would have been a word, muqtada too can be treated as a word and hence can be general or specific. The author then addresses an objection: a Hanafi example involving the statement 'Set free your slave on my behalf' appears to assume generality in muqtada, contradicting their principle. The response clarifies that the generality in that example arises not from the muqtada (the implied sale) but from the explicitly mentioned word 'your slave' in the underlying paraphrase: 'Sell your slave on my behalf, then be my agent for manumission.' Thus, the Hanafi position remains consistent. Following the theoretical discussion, the author applies the principle to a practical fiqh scenario: a person swears 'If I eat, my slave is free.' Here, 'food' (ta'am) is muqtada because eating without food is impossible. Since muqtada has neither generality nor particularity, the oath-breaker cannot specify a type of food; any food will cause the oath to be broken. This holds both in court (qada') and in private conscience (diyanah). Finally, an objection is raised: if ta'am has no generality, then why is the oath-breaker held liable for every food? The response would be that the lack of generality does not mean the scope is limited; rather, it means the muqtada is inherently indeterminate and covers all instances, but not as a result of general wording.

Key Terms
مقتضی (muqtada): An implied element required for the validity of a statement, not explicitly mentioned but understood from context.
عموم (umum): Generality of a word covering all its individuals without exception.
خصوص (khusus): Specificity, the restriction of a general word to a subset of its individuals.
محذوف (mahzuf): An omitted word that is understood from the context and considered like a spoken word in certain schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, does muqtada (the required element in speech) carry generality (umum)?
A) Yes, because it is like an omitted word.
B) No, because umum and khusus are attributes of words, while muqtada belongs to the category of meaning.
C) Yes, but only in certain cases.
D) No, but it can be particularized by intention.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis hold that muqtada, being a meaning not a word, does not admit generality or particularity, affecting oath rulings.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

Implied Terms (Muqtada) and Generality: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Explains the Hanafi position that muqtada has no generality or particularity, with fiqh examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on muqtada and explain why it differs from the Shafi'i view, using the oath example.

Core Idea
The implied (muqtada) has no generality or particularity—the oath breaks by any eating, not by food's generality.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is. The focus is on whether an implied term (muqtada) — something necessarily understood but not explicitly mentioned — carries generality (umum) that can be particularized. The key example is a person who swears an oath saying 'If I eat, then my slave is free.' The word 'food' is not stated but is implied. The dispute centers on whether the oath-breaker's intention to limit 'eating' to a specific food is valid in the court of conscience (diyanah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms muqtada, umum, takhsis, and nakirah in the Arabic text. Understand their definitions as they apply to this dispute.
Pay attention to how 'food' (ta'am) is implied from 'eating' (akl).
2
Analyze the Hanafi position
Read the Hanafi argument that muqtada has no generality or particularity. Note the distinction between the ruling in qada (judicial) vs diyanah (conscience).
Hanafis hold that the oath is broken by any food because the essence of eating exists in all foods, not because 'food' is general.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i position and its evidence
Read the Shafi'i counter-argument: the implied term is like the mentioned, and a nakirah in a conditional context is general. They permit particularization by intention.
Shafi'is rely on the principle 'al-mahdhuf ka al-madhkur' (the omitted is like the mentioned).
4
Contrast with explicit mention
The text contrasts the muqtada case with explicit mention of 'food' (ta'am). Identify the difference in ruling and the reason.
When 'food' is explicitly stated, all schools agree on generality and allow particularization.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: in the Hanafi view, the person is sinful (diyanatan) and must free the slave if he eats bread; in the Shafi'i view, his intention saves him from sin.
This shows how a usul principle directly affects fiqh rulings.

قوله حتى اذا قال ان اكلت فعبدی حر ونوى الخ آپ ماقبل میں یہ بات دلائل کی روشنی میں سمجھ چکے ہیں کہ احناف کے ہاں مقتضی میں عموم و خصوص حاری نہیں ہوتے جبکہ حضرت شوافع اس کے قائل ہیں۔ اب صاحب کتاب فقہی مسائل کی روشنی میں بطور مرچ یہی اصول واضح فرما ر ہے ہیں۔ (1) پہلی تفریح صورت مسئلہ فقیہ:۔۔۔ کسی شخص نے کہا ان اكلت فعبدی حر ظاہری بات ہے کہ اس کا کلام تبھی درست ہو گا جب بطور اقتناء لفظ طعاماً مقدر مانا جائے ۔ اب اگر یہ کہے کہ اس سے میں نے خاص قسم کے کھانے کی نیت کی ہے مثلاً ان اكلت اللحم فعبدی حر ، دوسرے کھانے مراد نہیں تو ایسے شخص کی تصدیق کی جائے گی یا نہیں۔ قضاء تو بالاتفاق اس کی نیت کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ یہ خلاف ظاہر کی نیت ہے جبکہ قاضی (شرع) ظاہر کا مکلف ہے۔ احناف کے ہاں دیا یہ بھی اس کی نیت معتبر نہیں : احناف کہتے ہیں کہ دیا یہ اس لئے اسکی نیت صحیح نہیں ہے کہ اس کے کلام میں لفظ طعالماً مذکور نہیں بلکہ یہ اکل کے اقتضاء سے مستفاد ہے کیونکہ اکل بدون طعام من نہیں اور مقتضی میں عموم نہیں ہوتا نہ خصوص ۔ اس لئے یہ جس قسم کا بھی کھانا کھانے حانث تصور ہوگا۔ خواہ نیت طعام دون طعام کی کیوں نہ کرے وجہ یہ ہے کہ جب طعام مقتضی میں عموم نہیں تو یہ قابل تخصیص بھی نہیں ہوگا ۔ وأما حنثه بكل طعام الخ. صاحب نور الانور شیخ احمد رحمہ اللہ ایک اعتراض کو دور کر رہے ہیں: اعتراض :- - جب آپ کہتے ہیں کہ اس صورت میں طعام میں عموم نہیں تو ہر قسم کا کھانا کھانے سے قائل کو حانث نہیں ہونا چاہئے ۔ جواب:۔۔۔ ہر طرح کا کھانا کھانے کی وجہ سے اس کا حث اس لئے نہیں کہ ہم نے طعام میں عموم مانا ہے بلکہ اس بناء پر ہے کہ ماہیت اکل ہر قسم کے کھانے میں موجود ہے۔ شوافع کے ہاں خاص طعام کی نیست دیا یہ درست ہے شوافع حضرات کا کہنا ہے کہ اگر اس نے طعام سے صرف گوشت کھانے کی صورت میں ترتب جزاء کی نیت کی تھی اور گوشت کے علاوہ کھانے کھائے ہیں تو یہ دیا یہ حانث نہیں ہوگا ۔ شوافع کی دلیل:۔۔۔ ان کے ہاں مختلفی محذوف کی طرح اور محذوف مذکور کی طرح ہے یہاں نمره یعنی طعام تحت الشرف واقع ہے اور ایسی صورتوں میں نکرہ عام ہوا کرتا ہے۔ نہر اطعام میں عموم ہے اور تخصیص کی نیت بھی درست ہے قوله : وان قال ان اكلت طعاما أو أكل الخ شاریخ ان مثالوں کے ذریعے یہ بیان کرنا چاہتے ہیں کہ مذکور میں عموم اور خصوص بالا تفاق جاری ہوتے ہیں ۔ مثال کے طور پر کسی شخص نے یوں کہا ان اكلت طعاما فعبدی حر ، يا بالفاظ دیگر اس طرح کہا لا اكل اكلا والا عبدى حر ان دونوں سورتوں میں اگر نیت نہیں کی تو ہر طرح کے کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا کیونکہ پہلی مثال میں طعاما اور دوسری مثال میں اکلا ، نہ کو ر ہے جس میں بوجہ نکرہ ہونے کے عموم ہے اور یہ اگر کسی خاص قسم کے کھانے کی نیت کرے تو دوسرے کھانے کھانے سے نہ وہ قضاء حانت وگانہ دیا یہ کیونکہ عام کی تخصیص جائز ہے نیت سے قبل طعام اور اکل عام تھے اب خاص کر دیے قوله : ولكن ايراد هذا المثال الخ شارع فرماتے ہیں بعض اصولین نے متقضی کے لئے شرعی ہونے کی قید لگائی ہے۔ ان کے مذھب پر یہ مثال اس لئے درست نہیں کہ یہ عقلی ہے آگئے مذھب مختار کی وضاحت ان الفاظ میں کی ہے
His statement: 'Until when he says: If I eat, then my slave is free, and he intends...' You have understood earlier through proofs that according to the Hanafis, generality and particularity do not apply to the implied (muqtada), while the Shafi'is affirm that. Now the author clarifies this principle with fiqhi examples. (1) First issue: A person said: 'If I eat, then my slave is free.' It is apparent that his speech is only correct when the word 'food' is considered implied by way of requirement (iqtiḍā'). Now if he says that he intended a specific type of food — for example, 'If I eat meat, then my slave is free' — meaning other foods are not intended, will his intention be accepted? In qaḍā (judicial ruling), there is consensus that his intention is not considered, because it is an intention contrary to the apparent meaning, and the judge (shar‘an) is bound by the apparent. According to the Hanafis, his intention is also not valid in dīyānah (conscience/religious duty). The Hanafis say: His intention is not correct because in his speech the word 'food' is not mentioned; rather, it is derived from the requirement of 'eating', because eating without food is impossible. And there is no generality or particularity in the implied (muqtada). Therefore, he is considered to have broken his oath by eating any kind of food, even if he intended food specifically [or not]. The reason is that when the implied 'food' has no generality, it cannot be particularized. 'And as for his breaking the oath by every food, etc.' The author of Nūr al-Anwār, Shaykh Aḥmad (may Allah have mercy on him), is addressing an objection: Objection: When you say that in this case there is no generality in 'food', then the person should not be considered to break the oath by eating all types of food. Answer: His breaking the oath by all types of food is not because we assume generality in 'food', but rather because the essence of 'eating' exists in every type of food. According to the Shafi'is, the intention for specific food is valid in dīyānah. The Shafi'is say: If he intended that the penalty should only apply if he eats meat, and he ate other foods, then he does not break the oath in dīyānah. The Shafi'is' proof: According to them, the omitted (maḥdhūf) is like the mentioned (madhkūr). Here the implied word, i.e., 'food', occurs in the context of a condition, and in such cases a nakirah (indefinite noun) is general. There is generality in 'food', and the intention of particularization is valid. His statement: 'And if he says: If I eat food, or eat...' The commentators intend through these examples to explain that generality and particularity apply in the mentioned (madhkūr) by consensus. For example, a person said: 'If I eat food, then my slave is free,' or in other words: 'I will not eat any eating, otherwise my slave is free.' In both these cases, if he does not intend, he breaks the oath by every food, because in the first example 'food' and in the second 'eating' is a nakirah which has generality due to being indefinite. And if he intends a specific type of food, then he does not break the oath by other foods in qaḍā nor in dīyānah, because particularization of a general term is permissible. Before intention, 'food' and 'eating' were general; now they become specific. His statement: 'But the presentation of this example...' The commentator says: Some uṣūliyyūn have restricted the implied to being shar‘ī (legal) [as opposed to rational]. According to their school, this example is not correct because it is rational. Then he clarifies the chosen school with these words.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding whether the implied (muqtada) carries generality (umum) and particularity (khushus). The context is an oath: a person says 'If I eat, then my slave is free.' The word 'food' is not explicitly mentioned but is necessarily implied because eating requires food. The question: if the person later claims he only intended a specific food (e.g., meat), is his intention valid? The author first confirms that in judicial ruling (qada), intention contrary to the apparent is not considered by consensus. However, the dispute is in dianah (religious/conscience). The Hanafis argue that the implied term (muqtada) has no generality or particularity; therefore, any eating breaks the oath regardless of intention. The author addresses an objection: if muqtada has no generality, why is the person held to break the oath by any food? The answer is that the breaking is due to the essence of 'eating' being present in all foods, not due to generality. The Shafi'is counter that the implied term is like the explicit term, and a nakirah in a conditional context is general. They permit particularization by intention. The passage then contrasts with the explicit mention of 'food' (ta'am) where all scholars agree on generality and the validity of particularization. The commentator notes that some usulis restrict the rule of muqtada to legal (shar'i) contexts, but the chosen Hanafi position applies to rational impliations as well. The passage serves to reinforce the Hanafi usul principle that muqtada lacks inherent generality, and that rulings based on muqtada are not subject to particularization.

Key Terms
مقتضی (muqtada): An implied meaning necessarily required by the wording, but not explicitly mentioned (e.g., 'food' is implied by 'eating').
عموم (umoom): Generality of a term covering all individuals or types within its scope.
تخصیص (takhsis): The act of specifying a general term to apply to only part of its individuals.
نکرہ (nakirah): An indefinite noun; in a conditional context, it is considered general by Shafi'is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person says 'If I eat, then my slave is free' and later intends only meat, what is the ruling in dianah (conscience) if he eats bread?
A) He breaks the oath and must free the slave.
B) He does not break the oath because he intended only meat.
C) The intention is accepted and he is not sinful.
D) It depends on the type of bread.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that muqtada has no generality or particularity means that in oaths, any act fulfilling the implied condition breaks the oath, regardless of intention.

Generality and Specification in Oaths: The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on 'Food'
Dispute on generality of indefinite in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite noun in a conditional context indicates generality, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument on specification by intention.

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in a conditional oath is general; intention can specify it, but the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on the effect of that specification.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute concerning oaths involving 'food' (ta'am). Both Hanafis and Shafi'is agree that an indefinite noun in a conditional context is general, but they diverge on whether the speaker's unexpressed intention to specify a particular type of food can restrict that generality. Understanding this disagreement reveals deeper principles about the operation of general and specific terms, as well as the role of implicature (muqtada) and omitted parts (mahzuf) in legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the ruling on oath-breaking when a general term is used with a specific intention. The core question: does the oath apply to all instances of the category, or only to what the speaker intended?
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that the general term is binding unless verbally specified; mere intention does not restrict. The basis is the common essence of eating (māhiyyat al-akl) present in all foods, not just linguistic generality.
The commentator emphasizes that the ruling is not based solely on assuming generality in food, but on the essence of eating.
3
Understand the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'is argue that the omitted part (mahzuf) is like the mentioned, and the indefinite in a conditional context is general but can be specified by intention. Thus, if the speaker intended only meat, other foods do not break the oath.
The Shafi'i proof: mahzuf is like mazkur; nakira in shart is general; specification by intention is valid.
4
Examine the underlying usul concepts
The passage also discusses muqtada (implicature) and mahzuf (omitted part). The preferred view is that muqtada can be both legal and rational, but mahzuf is always linguistic. This affects how we interpret implied conditions.
This part clarifies the broader context of the dispute and the commentator's preferred position.

جواب:۔۔۔ ہر طرح کا کھانا کھانے کی وجہ سے اس کا حث اس لئے نہیں کہ ہم نے طعام میں عموم مانا ہے بلکہ اس بناء پر ہے کہ ماہیت اکل ہر قسم کے کھانے میں موجود ہے۔ شوافع کے ہاں خاص طعام کی نیست دیا یہ درست ہے شوافع حضرات کا کہنا ہے کہ اگر اس نے طعام سے صرف گوشت کھانے کی صورت میں ترتب جزاء کی نیت کی تھی اور گوشت کے علاوہ کھانے کھائے ہیں تو یہ دیا یہ حانث نہیں ہوگا ۔ شوافع کی دلیل:۔۔۔ ان کے ہاں مختلفی محذوف کی طرح اور محذوف مذکور کی طرح ہے یہاں نمره یعنی طعام تحت الشرف واقع ہے اور ایسی صورتوں میں نکرہ عام ہوا کرتا ہے۔ نہر اطعام میں عموم ہے اور تخصیص کی نیت بھی درست ہے قوله : وان قال ان اكلت طعاما أو أكل الخ شاریخ ان مثالوں کے ذریعے یہ بیان کرنا چاہتے ہیں کہ مذکور میں عموم اور خصوص بالا تفاق جاری ہوتے ہیں ۔ مثال کے طور پر کسی شخص نے یوں کہا ان اكلت طعاما فعبدی حر ، يا بالفاظ دیگر اس طرح کہا لا اكل اكلا والا عبدى حر ان دونوں سورتوں میں اگر نیت نہیں کی تو ہر طرح کے کھانے سے حانث ہو جائے گا کیونکہ پہلی مثال میں طعاما اور دوسری مثال میں اکلا ، نہ کو ر ہے جس میں بوجہ نکرہ ہونے کے عموم ہے اور یہ اگر کسی خاص قسم کے کھانے کی نیت کرے تو دوسرے کھانے کھانے سے نہ وہ قضاء حانت وگانہ دیا یہ کیونکہ عام کی تخصیص جائز ہے نیت سے قبل طعام اور اکل عام تھے اب خاص کر دیے قوله : ولكن ايراد هذا المثال الخ شارع فرماتے ہیں بعض اصولین نے متقضی کے لئے شرعی ہونے کی قید لگائی ہے۔ ان کے مذھب پر یہ مثال اس لئے درست نہیں کہ یہ عقلی ہے آگئے مذھب مختار کی وضاحت ان الفاظ میں کی ہے قوله والاولى ان يقال الخ درست یہی ہے کہ مقتضی جس طرح شرعی ہو سکتا ہے اسی طرح عقلی بھی ہو سکتا ہے البتہ محذوف ہر صورت میں لغوی ہوتا ہے
Answer: ... It is not because we assumed generality in food, but because the essence of eating is present in every kind of food. Among the Shafi'is, specifying particular food is valid. The Shafi'is say: if he intended only meat in the penalty for eating food, and he ate other than meat, then he is not an oath-breaker. Shafi'i proof: With them, the omitted is like the mentioned, and here the indefinite (nakira) i.e. food occurs in the condition (shart), and in such cases the indefinite is general. There is generality in food (nakira), and specifying by intention is valid. He said: 'And if he said: If I eat food or eating...'. The commentator (sharih) wants to explain through these examples that generality and specificity in the mentioned are operative by agreement. For example, if someone said: 'If I eat food, then my slave is free', or in other words: 'I will not eat any eating, and if I do, my slave is free'. In both scenarios, if he did not intend, he becomes an oath-breaker from every kind of food because in the first example 'food' and in the second 'eating' are indefinite (nakira) which implies generality due to being indefinite. And if he intends a specific kind of food, then eating other kinds does not result in obligation of penalty because specification of a general is permissible; before intention, 'food' and 'eating' were general, now they are specified. He said: 'But introducing this example...' The commentator says: Some usul scholars have restricted the implicature (muqtada) to being legal (shar'i). According to their madhhab, this example is not valid because it is rational. He clarifies the chosen madhhab in these words: 'And the better is to say...' That is correct: just as muqtada can be legal, similarly it can be rational. However, the omitted part (mahzuf) is always linguistic (lughawi).
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage addresses the application of the principles of general (‘āmm) and specific (khāṣṣ) in the context of oaths (yamīn). The commentator explains that when someone utters a conditional oath involving eating food (e.g., 'If I eat food, my slave is free'), the word 'food' (ṭa‘ām) is an indefinite noun (nakira) in a conditional context, which by default indicates generality (umūm) according to both Hanafis and Shafi‘is. However, if the speaker specifically intended a particular type of food (e.g., meat), then only that type triggers the oath; other foods do not. This is because specification (takhṣīṣ) of a general term is permissible. The Shafi‘is agree but emphasize that the indefinite in a condition is general and can be specified by intention. The passage then transitions to a discussion of the concept of muqtaḍā (implicature). Some scholars restrict muqtaḍā to legal (shar‘ī) contexts, but the preferred view (al-mukhtār) is that it can be both legal and rational. However, the omitted element (maḥdhūf) is always linguistic. This dispute affects how we interpret implied conditions in oaths and other statements.

Key Terms
عام (ʿāmm): A general term that covers all individuals of its category; here applied to 'food' (ṭaʿām) in an oath.
خاص (khāṣṣ): A specific term or intention that restricts a general term; here, specifying a particular kind of food.
نکرہ (nakira): An indefinite noun; in conditional contexts, it indicates generality (ʿumūm).
مقتضی (muqtaḍā): Implicature; the implied meaning that must be assumed for the statement to be coherent.
محذوف (maḥdhūf): The omitted part of a statement that is understood implicitly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says, 'By Allah, I will not eat food' intending only meat, and then eats bread, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) He becomes an oath-breaker because bread is food.
B) He does not become an oath-breaker because he intended only meat.
C) He becomes an oath-breaker only if he ate meat.
D) He is excused because he did not specify in the oath.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun in a conditional oath is general, and mere intention does not restrict it; the Hanafi position is grounded in the common essence of eating, not just linguistic convention.

Validity of Intention of Three Divorces in Different Utterances
Comparing Hanafi and Shafi'i rulings on intention of three divorces in 'anti taliq' vs 'talliqi nafsak'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on intending three divorces in 'anti taliq' and explain how the concept of muqtada governs the dispute.

Core Idea
The scope of muqtada determines validity of intention: Hanafis deny generality, Shafi'is permit it.

This lesson explores a key usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the validity of intending a specific number of divorces when using utterances like 'anti taliq'. The disagreement centers on the concept of muqtada (implied requirement), specifically whether it can carry generality ('umum). Understanding this dispute illustrates how different usul principles lead to different fiqhi rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed utterances
Focus on 'anti taliq' and 'tallaqtuka'. These are khabar (informative statements). Compare with 'talliqi nafsak' (delegation) where both schools agree intention of three is valid.
The dispute is only over khabar utterances, not tafwid.
2
Understand the concept of muqtada
Muqtada is an implied requirement that makes speech meaningful. For khabar, the muqtada is that the reported event (divorce) existed prior to the statement.
Hanafis limit muqtada to the minimum needed; Shafi'is allow it to carry generality.
3
Follow the Hanafi argument
Hanafis argue that muqtada only gives the minimal meaning (one divorce), not generality. Thus intending three is invalid.
The key phrase: 'nahnu la naqul bi al-umum fi al-muqtada'.
4
Follow the Shafi'i argument
Shafi'is argue that muqtada can be either general or specific, so intending three is valid as a general implication.
They see no restriction on muqtada's scope.
5
Contrast with utterances where intention is valid
In 'talliqi nafsak', the utterance is not khabar but tafwid; no muqtada issue. In 'anti ba'in', the word ba'in is ambiguous between one and three divorces, so intention is simply choosing a meaning.
Both schools agree on these, but for different usul reasons.

میں فرد حکمی کا بھی احتمال ہوتا ہے اور نیت کے بعد فرد حکمی مراد لینا درست ہے لہذا تین طلاقوں کی نیت صحیح ہے کیونکہ یہ طلاق ( مصدر ) کا فرد کی ہے۔ (٢) " انت بائن" کی صورت میں اعتبار نیت کی تخریج شارخ فرماتے ہیں کہ بانس کے لفظ سے جو بیو یہ مستفاد ہے اس کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ (1) مینونت خفیفہ ( یعنی ایک طلاق) اور (۲) بینونت خلیفہ ( یعنی تین طلاق ) لہذا تین طلاقوں ( مینو یہ خلیفہ ) مبینونہ کی نسبت احد الاحتمالین کی نیت ہے لہند اور ست ہے۔ نکتہ: ۔۔۔۔۔ بعض علماء نے مائن رحمتہ اللہ کے قول على اختلاف التخريج ۔ مراد لیا ہے کہ ان مسائل کے دلائل میں احناف اور شوافع کی تخریج مختلف ہے اس صورت میں احناف کی تخریج تو مذکور ہو گئی شوافع کی تخریج کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ان کے ہاں یہ امثلہ بھی مقتضی کی قبیل سے ہیں ۔ لہذا میں عموم و خصوص جاری ہو سکتا ہے اور تین کی نیست گو یا عموم کی نیت ہے۔
His statement: 'And it is better to say, etc.' The lesson is that the muqtada (requirement) can be shar'i (legal) just as it can be 'aqli (rational), but the omitted element (mahzuf) is always lughawi (linguistic). His statement: 'And likewise, if he says 'anti taliq' or 'tallaqtuka' and intends three, it is not valid, etc.' This is the second elaboration. The issue: A man divorces his wife with the words 'anti taliq' or 'tallaqtuka'. If he intends three divorces, according to the Hanafis this intention is not valid, so three divorces do not occur. The Hanafi proof: The commentator (Sharh) stated the Hanafi proof that the husband's utterances 'anti taliq' and 'tallaqtuka' are khabar (informative statements), and the nature of khabar requires that the thing informed about (mukhbar 'anhu, i.e., divorce) exists before the statement. Therefore, the husband's speech can only be meaningful if divorce is assumed to exist prior, even if he did not actually divorce before. It is better than considering the speech of a sane adult Muslim, which is correct Arabic, as meaningless, so we assume that divorce occurred prior by way of muqtada, as if the husband said: 'You are divorced because I divorced you before this.' The reason that intending three is not valid in these two utterances is that we do not hold generality (umum) in muqtada. The Shafi'i school: According to the Shafi'is, it is valid to intend two or three divorces in such speech, so the intention is considered. The Shafi'i proof: These two utterances indicate divorce, and the proof of ta'liq (suspension) is by way of muqtada, in which it is permissible to apply both generality and particularity. His statement: 'Contrary to his statement: 'talliqi nafsak' or 'anti ba'in', etc.' Explanation: In this expression, he mentions as a precautionary example that if the husband, instead of 'anti taliq' or 'tallaqtuka', divorces with these words: (1) 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself) or (2) 'anti ba'in' (you are irrevocably separated), and intends three, this is valid by consensus. However, the proofs of the two schools are different, although the ruling is the same. (1) Derivation of validity of intention in the case of 'talliqi nafsak': Unlike the previous cases, this speech is not khabar but rather tafwid (delegation), and it does not require divorce (the masdar) as muqtada; rather, its indication is by way of language, and the masdar has a real individual (fard haqiqi), and also a legal individual (fard hukmi) is possible. After intention, taking the legal individual is valid, so the intention of three is valid because it is an individual of the masdar 'talaq'. (2) Derivation of validity of intention in the case of 'anti ba'in': The commentator says that what is derived from the word 'ba'in' for the wife is of two types: (a) minor separation (i.e., one divorce) and (b) major separation (i.e., three divorces). Therefore, intending three divorces (major separation) is intending one of the two probabilities, so it is valid. Point: Some scholars took Mayan Rahmatullah's statement 'ala ikhtilaf al-takhrij' to mean that the proofs of these issues differ between Hanafis and Shafi'is. The Hanafi derivation has been mentioned; the Shafi'i derivation is that, according to them, these examples also belong to the category of muqtada, so generality and particularity can be applied, and intending three is like intending generality.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is an usul dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is concerning the validity of intending a number of divorces when using certain pronouncements. The key issue revolves around the concept of 'muqtada' (implied requirement). The Hanafis argue that when a husband says 'anti taliq' or 'tallaqtuka', these are khabar (informative statements) that imply the prior existence of divorce. However, they do not accept that the muqtada includes generality ('umum), meaning the statement cannot carry the implication of multiple divorces. Therefore, an intention of three is invalid. The Shafi'is, by contrast, hold that such statements do imply divorce and that the muqtada can accommodate both generality and particularity, so intending three is valid because it is a particular specification. The passage then contrasts this with phrases like 'talliqi nafsak' (divorce yourself) and 'anti ba'in' (you are irrevocably separated). In these cases, both schools agree that intending three is valid, but for different reasons. 'Talliqi nafsak' is not khabar but tafwid (delegation), so it directly indicates divorce without requiring a prior existence, and the word 'talaq' itself can apply to multiple instances. 'Anti ba'in' is ambiguous between minor and major separation, and intending three is simply choosing one of the possible meanings. The disagreement highlights how different usul principles—particularly the nature of muqtada and its capacity for generalization—lead to different fiqhi rulings.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtada): The implied requirement that makes speech meaningful, e.g., assuming prior divorce to give meaning to 'anti taliq'.
خبر (khabar): An informative statement that reports about something, requiring its existence prior to the statement.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation, as in 'talliqi nafsak', where the husband authorizes the wife to divorce herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the intention of three divorces invalid when the husband says 'anti taliq'?
A) Because 'anti taliq' is not a clear statement of divorce.
B) Because muqtada (implied requirement) does not carry generality ('umum).
C) Because the wife must consent to three divorces.
D) Because 'anti taliq' implies only one divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on intending three divorces in 'anti taliq' hinges on the principle that muqtada does not include generality, while the Shafi'i position allows it.

Intending Three Divorces with Ambiguous Phrases: Hanafi vs Shafi'i Derivation
Compares Hanafi and Shafi'i derivations for ambiguous divorce phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi usul principles (tafwid and ihtimal) to derive the validity of intending multiple divorces with ambiguous phrases, and compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i derivations.

Core Idea
With ambiguous divorce phrases, intention determines the number because the words have multiple plausible meanings.

This passage explores a key usul dispute: when a husband uses phrases like 'Divorce yourself' or 'You are bain' and intends three divorces, all schools agree it is valid, but their derivations differ. Understanding this difference sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi usul categories such as tafwid (delegation) and ihtimal (linguistic possibility) versus the Shafi'i concept of muqtada (implied necessity).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ambiguous phrases
Read the Arabic phrases 'طلقی نفسك' (divorce yourself) and 'انت بائن' (you are bain). Note that they are not explicit like 'أنت طالق'.
2
Understand the Hanafi derivation for 'طلقی نفسك'
Recognize that this phrase is tafwid (delegation), not khabar (statement). The word 'divorce' (مصدر) can have multiple instances; intention specifies the number.
3
Understand the Hanafi derivation for 'انت بائن'
The word 'bain' indicates baynunah (separation), which has two types: minor (one divorce) and major (three). Intention selects one of these possible meanings.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i derivation
Shafi'is classify both phrases under muqtada (implied necessity) and apply generality (umum) – the intention of three is like intending generality.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Although the ruling is the same, the methodological difference affects other cases. Hanafis use tafwid and ihtimal, while Shafi'is rely on muqtada and umum.

قوله : بخلاف قوله : طلقي نفسك او انت بائن الخ
تشریح عبارت؛
اس عبارت میں احترازی مثال کے طور پر ذکر کیا ہے کہ اگر شوہر انت طالق یا طلقتک کی بجائے اگر ان الفاظ سے طلاق دے (۱) طلقی نفسک (۲) یا انت بائن اور تین طلاقوں کی نیت کرے تو یہ بالاتفاق جائز ہے البتہ دونوں مسلوں کی دلیلیں مختلف ہیں اگر چہ حکم ایک ہے۔ (۱) طلقی نفسک " کی صورت میں اعتبار نیت کی تخریج :

شارخ فرماتے ہیں کہ بائن کے لفظ سے جو بینونت مستفاد ہے اس کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔ (1) بینونت خفیفہ ( یعنی ایک طلاق) اور (۲) بینونت خلیفہ ( یعنی تین طلاق ) لہذا تین طلاقوں ( بینونت خلیفہ ) کی نیت احد الاحتمالین کی نیت ہے لہذا درست ہے۔

He said: As opposed to his saying: 'Divorce yourself' or 'You are bain' etc. Explanation of the phrase: In this phrase, he mentions as a cautionary example that if the husband divorces with these words instead of 'You are divorced' or 'I have divorced you' – (1) 'Divorce yourself' (2) or 'You are bain' and intends three divorces, then it is unanimously valid. However, the proofs of the two schools differ although the ruling is one. (1) In the case of 'Divorce yourself', the derivation of the validity of intention: Unlike the previous cases, this speech is not a statement (khabar) but a delegation (tafwid), and in it, divorce (the infinitive) is not an implied necessity (muqtada), rather it is by linguistic indication, and the infinitive has one real instance (fard haqiqi) and also it can have a legal instance (fard hukmi). After intention, taking the legal instance is correct, so the intention of three divorces is valid because it is an instance of the infinitive. (2) In the case of 'You are bain', the derivation of the validity of intention: The commentator (Sharh) says that the separation (baynunah) derived from the word 'bain' is of two types: (1) minor separation (baynunah sughra – i.e., one divorce) and (2) major separation (baynunah kubra – i.e., three divorces). Therefore, intending three divorces (major separation) is intending one of the two possibilities, so it is correct. Note: Some scholars have understood the author's statement 'with a difference in derivation' to mean that in these issues, the derivation of the Hanafis and Shafi'is differs. In this case, the Hanafi derivation has been mentioned. The gist of the Shafi'i derivation is that according to them, these examples also fall under the category of implied necessity (muqtada), so generality and specificity can be applied, and the intention of three is like intending generality.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of a ruling regarding divorce phrases that are ambiguous. The author distinguishes between explicit phrases like 'You are divorced' (which do not allow intention to increase the number of divorces) and ambiguous phrases like 'Divorce yourself' and 'You are bain' (which do allow intention). The ruling is that if a husband says 'Divorce yourself' or 'You are bain' and intends three divorces, all scholars agree it is valid. However, the reasoning differs between the Hanafis and Shafi'is. The Hanafi reasoning depends on linguistic analysis: 'Divorce yourself' is a delegation (tafwid), not a statement, so it does not necessitate a specific number; the word 'divorce' can refer to one or more, and intention clarifies it. For 'You are bain', the word 'bain' itself has two possible meanings (one or three divorces), so intention selects one. The note clarifies that Shafi'is consider both phrases as falling under the category of implied necessity (muqtada) where generality and specificity can be applied, so intention of three is like intending generality. The passage exemplifies how Hanafi usul principles like linguistic plausibility (ihtimal) and intention (niyyah) operate in fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
تفویض (tafwid): A speech that delegates the determination of number or type to the intention of the speaker.
بینونت (baynunah): The state of being separated after divorce; two levels: minor (one revocable divorce) and major (three irrevocable divorces).
مقتضی (muqtada): A speech that necessarily implies a particular meaning by its very nature, without requiring external evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because 'بائن' indicates baynunah, which has two known types; intention selects one.
C) Because the phrase is an oath that requires fulfillment.
D) Because the husband's intention always overrides the literal meaning.

Key Takeaway
When a divorce phrase is ambiguous (like 'طلقی نفسک' or 'انت بائن'), intention determines the number, as per Hanafi usul.

First Invalid Argument: Specification by Proper Noun
Explains the first invalid argument: proper noun specification does not imply exclusive ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first invalid argument (specification by proper noun) and explain why Hanafis reject its absolute application in favor of considering the underlying 'illah.

Core Idea
Proper noun specification does not automatically restrict a ruling—the underlying cause ('illah) may generalize it.

In this passage, the author introduces a section on arguments that appear valid but are actually flawed (wujuh fasida). The first such argument is the claim that when a ruling is attached to a specific proper noun, that ruling is exclusive to the named entity. This principle, advocated by some Ash'aris and Hanbalis, is rejected by Hanafis who prioritize the legal cause behind the ruling. The example of the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' is analyzed to show how the Ansar's literal understanding was later refined by considering the broader cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid argument
Recognize the claim that specification by a proper noun (ism 'alam) necessarily excludes all other cases.
This is the first of several 'weak arguments' (wujuh fasida) that the author will refute.
2
Understand the opposing view
Some Ash'aris and Hanbalis hold that when a ruling is explicitly attached to a proper noun, it is restricted to that named entity.
They cite the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' as proof, where the second 'water' is interpreted as semen.
3
Analyze the evidence
The hadith is used by the Ansar to conclude that ghusl is only obligatory upon ejaculation (the named case), not mere insertion.
This shows how the principle of takhsis (specification) was applied in practice.
4
Recognize the Hanafi critique
Hanafis argue that the ruling is based on the cause ('illah) – sexual impurity – not the specific word. Thus, the ruling can be generalized via qiyas.
The refutation is cut off in this passage but typically relies on istihsan and broader maqasid.

قوله ثم لما كانت تمسكات ابي حنيفه الخ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ حضرت امام اعظم ابو حنیفہ کے ہاں تو استدلال ان ہی چار اقسام سے ہوتا ہے جو مفصل بیان ہوئیں تا ہم بعض حضرات بالخصوص شوافع کے ان کے علاوہ بھی دلائل ہیں ، جو اس قصد سے ذکر کئے جاتے ہیں کہ ان کی تحقیق کی جائے اور ان کی کمزوری اور فساد کو دلائل سے ثابت کیا جائے ۔
قوله فصل : التنصيص على الشئى باسمه العلم يدل على الخصوص الخ
کسی شئے کی اس کے علم کے ساتھ صراحت تخصیص کا فائدہ دیتی ہے۔
یہاں سے تمسکات نیفہ نا مدد میں سے پہلی وجہ فاسد کا بیان ہے، جس کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ بعض اشاعر د اور حنابلہ کے ہاں یہ قاعدہ ہے کہ اگر کسی چیز کو علم ( ہر لفظ جوزات پر دال ہو خواہ علم ہو یا اہم جنس) کے ساتھ بالقصہ بیچ ذکر کیا جائے تو یہ اس کی تخصیص پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ یہ تم اس اسم علم کے ساتھ خاص ہے اور اسکے علاوہ میں نہیں پایا جائے گا۔
كقوله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الماء من الماء الخ
وجہ فاسد کی مثال کے ذریعے وضاحت:
آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم کا ارشاد ہے الماء من الماء آمیں الماء اول سے مراد نسل اور الماء ثانی سے مرادمنی ہے۔ یہاں اسم علم ( الماء ثانی یعنی خروج منی ) پر نسل کا حکم صراحتا لگایا گیا ہے لہذا انصار صحابہ کرام رضی اللہ تھم تخصیص کے اصول پر صرف خروج منی کی صورت میں وجوب غسل کے قائل تھے۔ لہذاوہ اکسال اخراج الذكر قبل الانزال ) کی صورت میں غسل کے قائل نہ تھے ۔ قاعد ہندکورہ کے قائلین انصار کے اس طرز سے استدلال کرتے ہیں کہ اگر تخصیص
He said: Then, since the arguments (tamassukat) of Abu Hanifa... the author says that Imam Abu Hanifa's inference is from only four categories which have been explained in detail; however, some people, especially the Shafi'is, have other proofs besides these, which are mentioned with the purpose that they be examined and their weakness and invalidity be proven through evidence. He said: Chapter: Specifying a thing by its proper noun indicates exclusivity. Specifying something with its name (proper noun) gives the benefit of exclusivity. From here begins the explanation of the first invalid argument among the arguments of the opponents (or perhaps 'beneficial' arguments). Its summary is that some Ash'aris and Hanbalis have the rule that if something is mentioned with explicit specificity by its proper noun (any word that indicates a specific entity, whether a proper noun or a common noun that specifies), then it indicates that it is specific to that proper noun and will not be found in anything else. Like the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Water is from water.' Elaboration through an example of the invalid argument: The Prophet said: 'Water is from water' – the first 'water' means the bath (ghusl) and the second 'water' means semen. Here, the ruling of obligation of bath is explicitly attached to the proper noun (the second 'water', i.e., emission of semen). Therefore, the Ansar (companions), may Allah be pleased with them, on the principle of exclusivity, believed that ghusl is only obligatory in the case of emission of semen. Hence, they did not consider ghusl obligatory in the case of mere insertion without emission. The proponents of this rule use this reasoning of the Ansar to argue that if exclusivity... (text cut off).

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on invalid arguments (wujuh fasida). The author first notes that while Imam Abu Hanifa's methodology relies on four types of proofs (previously explained), other scholars, particularly Shafi'is, present additional arguments that must be examined and refuted. The first invalid argument is from some Ash'aris and Hanbalis who claim that when a ruling is attached to a specific proper noun (ism 'alam), that ruling is exclusive to that named entity and does not apply to other similar cases. They support this with the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' (water is from water), where the second 'water' refers specifically to semen. The Ansar understood this to mean that ghusl is only wajib due to ejaculation, not mere insertion. The text is cut off, but the author presumably goes on to refute this principle. The passage illustrates how the Hanafi school rejects overly literal readings that ignore underlying legal causes ('illah) and broader purposes.

Key Terms
اسم علم (ism 'alam): A proper noun or a specific name that indicates a particular entity or individual.
تخصیص (takhsis): The act of specifying or limiting a ruling to a particular instance or entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Refuting the Argument that Proper Noun Implies Exclusivity
Usul dispute: refuting the argument that proper noun implies exclusivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the principle that specifying by proper noun indicates exclusivity, explain why Hanafis reject it using the Kalimah argument, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf in the ghusl ruling.

Core Idea
A proper noun does not negate other possibilities; otherwise, 'Muhammad Rasulullah' would deny all other prophets.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: does explicitly mentioning something with a proper noun restrict the ruling to that specific entity? Some Ash'aris and Hanbalis argue yes, citing the hadith 'الماء من الماء' and the practice of the Ansar Companions. The Hanafi response is devastating: if accepted, the Kalimah 'Muhammad Rasulullah' would imply no other messenger exists, which is kufr. Understanding this refutation sharpens your ability to evaluate claims about exclusivity in legal texts and recognize the broader implications of textual interpretation.

Guided Path
1
State the Opposing Principle
Articulate the principle that some scholars hold: if a ruling is explicitly attached to a proper noun (ism alam), it indicates that the ruling is exclusive to that specific thing and does not apply to others.
The term 'proper noun' here includes generic nouns used specifically, as in 'الماء' in the hadith.
2
Identify the Opposing Proof
Locate the evidence the opponents use: the hadith 'الماء من الماء' and the understanding of the Ansar Companions, who considered ghusl wajib only upon emission of semen (the second 'water').
The opponents argue that native speakers' practice supports the principle.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Refutation
Examine the core Hanafi counter-argument: if this principle were universally valid, then 'Muhammad Rasulullah' would mean that only Muhammad is a messenger, which is false and constitutes kufr. Therefore the principle cannot be correct.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument (dalil aqli).
4
Recognize the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Understand the practical consequence: Hanafis hold that ghusl is wajib for any sexual fluid or intercourse (even without emission), while opponents would restrict it to semen only.
The difference arises from accepting or rejecting the exclusivity principle.
5
Consider the Broader Significance
Note that the Hanafi response applies to both numbered and unnumbered cases: numbers may indicate emphasis, not exclusivity. Also anticipate the objection that this principle is used in juristic traditions, which the commentator proceeds to address.
The debate touches on the nature of 'amm and khass and the role of istihsan.

قوله ثم لما كانت تمسكات ابي حنيفه الخ مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ حضرت امام اعظم ابو حنیفہ کے ہاں تو استدلال ان ہی چار اقسام سے ہوتا ہے جو مفصل بیان ہوئیں تا ہم بعض حضرات بالخصوص شوافع کے ان کے علاوہ بھی دلائل ہیں ، جو اس قصد سے ذکر کئے جاتے ہیں کہ ان کی تحقیق کی جائے اور ان کی کمزوری اور فساد کو دلائل سے ثابت کیا جائے ۔ قوله فصل : التنصيص على الشئى باسمه العلم يدل على الخصوص الخ کسی شئے کی اس کے علم کے ساتھ صراحت تخصیص کا فائدہ دیتی ہے۔ یہاں سے تمسکات نیفہ نا مدد میں سے پہلی وجہ فاسد کا بیان ہے، جس کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ بعض اشاعر د اور حنابلہ کے ہاں یہ قاعدہ ہے کہ اگر کسی چیز کو علم ( ہر لفظ جوزات پر دال ہو خواہ علم ہو یا اہم جنس) کے ساتھ بالقصہ بیچ ذکر کیا جائے تو یہ اس کی تخصیص پر دلالت کرتا ہے کہ یہ تم اس اسم علم کے ساتھ خاص ہے اور اسکے علاوہ میں نہیں پایا جائے گا۔ كقوله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم الماء من الماء الخ وجہ فاسد کی مثال کے ذریعے وضاحت: آپ صلى الله عليه وسلم کا ارشاد ہے الماء من الماء آمیں الماء اول سے مراد نسل اور الماء ثانی سے مرادمنی ہے۔ یہاں اسم علم ( الماء ثانی یعنی خروج منی ) پر نسل کا حکم صراحتا لگایا گیا ہے لہذا انصار صحابہ کرام رضی اللہ تھم تخصیص کے اصول پر صرف خروج منی کی صورت میں وجوب غسل کے قائل تھے۔ لہذاوہ اکسال اخراج الذكر قبل الانزال ) کی صورت میں غسل کے قائل نہ تھے ۔ قاعد ہندکورہ کے قائلین انصار کے اس طرز سے استدلال کرتے ہیں کہ اگر تخصیص على الشيئ باسمه العلم کے تخصیص کا فائدہ حاصل نہ ہوتا تو انصار صحا رضى ان همر جو اہل زبان تھے حکم کیوں لگاتے تھے۔
وجہ فاسد کے فساد کی وجہ اول : اگر اس قاعدہ کو مان لیا جائے تو کلمہ طیبہ کے دوسرے جزء محمد رسول الله نعوذ باللہ کذب بلکہ کفر لازم آئے گا۔ کیونکہ یہاں بھی حکم رسالت اسم علم پر لگایا گیا ہے۔ چنانچہ معنی یہ ہوگا کہ محمہ کے علاوہ اور کوئی شخص رسالت کے مرتب علیا پر فائز نہیں۔ یہ سرا سرا کذب اور کفر ہے کیونکہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے سینکڑوں رسول بھیجے ہیں اور سب پر ایمان لانا شرط ایمان ہے امـــنـــت بالله وملئكته وكتبه ورسله الخ لہذا اس طرح حکم لگانے سے قاعدہ کی نفی نہیں ہوگی چاہے: (۱) یہ حکم کسی عدد کے ساتھ مقترن ہو مثلاً یہ حديث: خمس من الفواسق يقتلن في الحل والحرام الحداة والفارة والكلب العقور والحية و العقرب . نکتہ:۔۔۔۔ بعض حضرات اس صورت میں ماعدا کی نفی کا قول کرتے ہیں ان کا کہنا ہے کہ اگرنس سے ماعدا کی نفی مراد نہ لی جائے تو عدد کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں رہتا اس کا احناف نے یہ جواب دیا ہے کہ عدد کے ذکر سے مختلف مقاصد ہوتے ہیں یہاں اس کا مقصود زیادت اہتمام ہے لہذا یہ عدد کی نفی پر دلالت نہ کرنے کے باوجود مفید ہے۔ (۲) یا حکم عدد کے ساتھ مقترن نہ ہو جیسے متن والی مثال: الماء من الماء (الحديث) قوله لكن افتى المتأخرون الخ اس عبارت سے شارخ ایک اعتراض مقدر کا جواب دے رہے ہیں اعتراض:- آپ کا یہ کہنا تنصيص على الشني باسمه العلم نمی ماندا پر دلالت نہیں کرتی کس طرح درست ہو سکتا ہے حالانکہ یہ قاعدہ روایات فقہیہ میں جاری ہوتا ہے
The author says: Imam Abu Hanifa only uses those four types of proofs which have been detailed, but some people, especially the Shafi'is, have other proofs besides them, which are mentioned for the purpose of investigating them and proving their weakness and invalidity through evidence. He says: Section: Specifying something by its proper noun indicates exclusivity. Explicitly mentioning something with its proper noun gives the benefit of exclusivity. From here begins the explanation of the first invalid argument among the invalid proofs. Its summary is that some of the Ash'aris and Hanbalis hold this principle: if something is mentioned explicitly with a proper noun (every word that indicates upon an individual, whether a proper noun or a generic noun), it indicates its exclusivity, meaning that this ruling is specific to this proper noun and will not be found in others. For example, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Water is from water.' Clarification through the example of the invalid argument: The Prophet said 'Water is from water' – here the first 'water' means ghusl and the second 'water' means semen. Here the ruling of ghusl is explicitly applied to the proper noun (second water, i.e., emission of semen). Therefore, the Ansar Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) on the basis of the principle of exclusivity, believed in the obligation of ghusl only in the case of emission of semen. Hence they did not believe in ghusl in the case of [other types of?] exit of the penis before ejaculation. The proponents of this principle argue from this method of the Ansar: if specifying by proper noun did not give the benefit of exclusivity, then the Ansar Companions, who were native speakers, would not have ruled [that way]. The first cause of the invalidity of the invalid argument: If this principle is accepted, then the second part of the Kalimah Tayyibah, 'Muhammad Rasulullah' (may Allah protect us), would necessitate falsehood, rather disbelief. Because here also the ruling of messengership is applied to a proper noun. Thus the meaning would be that no one other than Muhammad is vested with the lofty rank of messengership. This is outright false and disbelief, because Allah Almighty sent hundreds of messengers and believing in all of them is a condition of faith: 'I believe in Allah and His angels and His books and His messengers' etc. Therefore, applying the ruling in this way does not negate the principle whether (1) the ruling is accompanied by a number, e.g., this hadith: 'Five pests are to be killed in the state of ihram and in the sacred territory: the kite, the mouse, the aggressive dog, the snake, and the scorpion.' Note: Some people say that in this case negation of others is intended; they say that if negation of others is not intended from the number, then the number has no benefit. The Hanafis have replied that mentioning a number serves various purposes; here its purpose is extra emphasis, so it is beneficial even without indicating negation. (2) Or the ruling is not accompanied by a number, like the example in the text: 'Water is from water' (the hadith). He says: 'But the later scholars gave fatwa...' With this phrase the commentator is responding to an anticipated objection: Objection: How can your statement that 'specifying something by its proper noun does not indicate exclusivity' be correct, while this principle is operative in juristic traditions?
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary. It presents and refutes the first invalid argument (وجه فاسد) that some scholars (Ash'aris and Hanbalis) use against the Hanafi methodology. The argument is: if a ruling is explicitly mentioned with a proper noun (اسم علم), it indicates that the ruling is exclusive to that particular thing and does not apply to others. The example given is the hadith 'الماء من الماء' (Water is from water), where 'water' (الماء الثاني) refers to semen, and the ruling (ghusl) is tied to it. The Ansar Companions understood this exclusively. However, the Hanafi commentator refutes this principle by showing a devastating consequence: if accepted, the Kalimah 'Muhammad Rasulullah' would imply that only Muhammad (peace be upon him) is a messenger, which is false and indeed kufr. Thus the principle is invalid. The commentator further discusses two scenarios: (1) when the ruling is accompanied by a number (e.g., the hadith about five pests), some argue that the number necessitates negation of others; Hanafis respond that the number is for emphasis, not exclusivity. (2) When no number is present, as in the original example. The commentator then addresses an anticipated objection that this principle is used in juristic traditions, implying its validity. The response (though cut off) likely explains that the principle is not universally applicable. Throughout, the Hanafi position is that specifying by proper noun does not negate other possibilities; it may indicate emphasis or the primary instance.

Key Terms
اسم علم (ism alam): A proper noun that denotes a specific individual or thing; in this context, any word that indicates a particular entity (whether a proper name or a generic noun used specifically).
تخصیص (takhsis): Specification or exclusivity; the idea that a ruling applies only to what is mentioned and not to others.
تنصيص (tansis): Explicit mention or specification in the text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The hadith 'الماء من الماء' is weak

Key Takeaway
Explicit mention by proper noun does not automatically exclude other possibilities; otherwise core tenets of faith would be undermined.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Does Specification by a Proper Noun Negate All Others?
Defends Hanafi principle on proper noun specification against objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi principle that specifying by a proper noun does not imply negation of unmentioned cases, and apply it to the hadith of five harmful creatures to distinguish between emphasis and limitation.

Core Idea
Specifying by a proper noun merely highlights the specified; it does not negate the unstated.

This lesson tackles a fundamental debate in Hanafi usul: when a text mentions a specific item (by name or number), does it automatically exclude everything else? The Hanafi school holds that specification by a proper noun or number does not imply negation of others, unless there is additional evidence. We will explore the proofs, address objections, and see the practical impact on fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi principle
Identify the core claim: specifying something by its proper noun (or number) only indicates the ruling for that specific entity; it remains silent on others, and silence does not imply negation.
This is the starting point of the dispute.
2
Analyze the opposing argument
Understand the objection: if specification did not negate others, it would be pointless. The opponent relies on the benefit of bringing up a specific example, assuming that mentioning one thing excludes all else.
Common intuition, but not always valid in usul.
3
Examine the Hanafi response and dalil
The Hanafis refute by giving linguistic examples (e.g., 'Zaid came' only tells about Zaid) and emphasizing that numbers can be for emphasis, not limitation. They distinguish between fiqh narrations (where such specification may negate by convention) and sacred texts (where it does not).
Key distinction: 'fiqh narrations' vs 'sacred texts'.
4
Apply to the hadith example
Take the hadith 'Five harmful creatures are killed...' and decide: does it limit killing to those five? According to Hanafi view, no; the number is for emphasis, and other harmful creatures may also be killed.
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الشيئ باسمه العلم کے تخصیص کا فائدہ حاصل نہ ہوتا تو انصار صحابہ رضی اللہ عنہم جو اہل زبان تھے حکم کیوں لگاتے تھے۔
وجہ فاسد کے فساد کی وجہ اول :
الله
اگر اس قاعدہ کو مان لیا جائے تو کلمہ طیبہ کے دوسرے جزء محمد رسول الله نعوذ باللہ کذب بلکہ کفر لازم آئے گا۔ کیونکہ یہاں بھی حکم رسالت اسم علم پر لگایا گیا ہے۔ چنانچہ معنی یہ ہوگا کہ محمد کے علاوہ اور کوئی شخص رسالت کے مرتبہ عالیہ پر فائز نہیں۔ یہ سراسر کذب اور کفر ہے کیونکہ اللہ تعالیٰ نے سینکڑوں رسول بھیجے ہیں اور سب پر ایمان لانا شرط ایمان ہے آمنا بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله الخ لہذا اس طرح حکم لگانے سے قاعدہ کی نفی نہیں ہوگی چاہے:
(۱) یہ حکم کسی عدد کے ساتھ مقترن ہو مثلاً یہ حدیث: خمس من الفواسق يقتلن في الحل والحرام: الحداة والفارة والكلب العقور والحية والعقرب.
نکتہ:
بعض حضرات اس صورت میں ماعدا کی نفی کا قول کرتے ہیں ان کا کہنا ہے کہ اگر نص سے ماعدا کی نفی مراد نہ لی جائے تو عدد کا کوئی فائدہ نہیں رہتا اس کا احناف نے یہ جواب دیا ہے کہ عدد کے ذکر سے مختلف مقاصد ہوتے ہیں یہاں اس کا مقصود زیادت اہتمام ہے لہذا یہ عدد کی نفی پر دلالت نہ کرنے کے باوجود مفید ہے۔
(۲) یا حکم عدد کے ساتھ مقترن نہ ہو جیسے متن والی مثال: الماء من الماء (الحديث) قوله لكن افتى المتأخرون الخ
اس عبارت سے شارح ایک اعتراض مقدر کا جواب دے رہے ہیں
اعتراض: آپ کا یہ کہنا تنصيص على الشيء باسمه العلم نمی ماعدا پر دلالت نہیں کرتی کس طرح درست ہو سکتا ہے حالانکہ یہ قاعدہ روایات فقہیہ میں جاری ہوتا ہے
جواب:
متاخرین علماء کا فتوی اس بات پر ہے کہ روایات فقہی میں تو یہ قاعدہ جاری ہوگا لیکن نصوص شرعیہ میں نہیں مثلاً صاحب ہدایہ نے مدیر عظیم کی یہ تعریف کی ہے کہ اس کی ایک جانب کو حرکت دینے سے جانب آخر میں حرکت کا اثر محسوس نہ ہو آگے فرمایا ہے جاز الوضوء من الجانب الأخر (یعنی جس جانب میں نجاست نہیں گری اس جانب سے وضو جائز ہے ) اس سے یہی معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ وقوع نجاست والی جانب سے وضو کرنا جائز نہیں۔
قوله لان النص لم يتناولة فكيف الخ.
احناف کی دلیل:
جب نص سے صرف انہی حکم معلوم ہو رہا ہے تو کس طرح غیر منصوص کو ثابت کیا جاسکتا ہے کیونکہ اگر نص اثبات کے متعلق ہے تو نفی غیر منصوص ہے اور نفی صرف منصوص حکم کو شامل ہوتی ہے نہ کہ غیر منصوص کو۔
مثال سے وضاحت
شارح فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی کہے: جاء نی زید تو یہ کلام صرف محبت زید پر دلالت کرتا ہے، رہا مرد کا آنا یا نہ آنا، یہ اس کے متعلق ساکت ہے۔ اسی طرح الماء من الماء اخراج منی سے وجوب غسل پر تو نص ہے رہا اکسال سے وجوب یا عدم وجوب غسل ، اس کے متعلق یہ ساکت ہے اور اکس سال سے عمل دوسری حدیث سے معلوم ہوتا ہے اذا جاوز الختان الختان فقد وجب الغسل انزل اولم ينزل۔ اس طرح خمس من الفواسق الخ اسکے علاوہ جانوروں کا حکم معلوم نہیں ہوتا۔
قوله : وفائدة التخصيص الخ :
اعتراض: ----- جب اسم علم کی صراحت ماعدا کی نفی میں مفید نہیں تو اس کی صراحت کا کیا
If specification by a proper noun did not yield the benefit of [negating] others, then why would the Companion Ansar, who were native speakers, derive rulings? ... First reason for the corruption of the corrupt argument: If this principle is accepted, then the second part of the kalimah, 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' — God forbid — would necessitate falsehood, rather unbelief. Because here too the ruling of prophethood is attached to a proper noun. Thus the meaning would be that no one other than Muhammad attains the high rank of prophethood. This is sheer falsehood and unbelief, because Allah sent hundreds of messengers and believing in all is a condition of faith. ... Therefore, applying the rule here does not negate the principle, whether: (1) the ruling is accompanied by a number, e.g., the hadith: 'Five from the harmful creatures are killed in the state of ihram and outside it: the kite, the mouse, the rabid dog, the snake, and the scorpion.' ... Some people claim that in this case the negation of others is intended. They say that if the negation of others is not taken from the text, then the number has no benefit. The Hanafis have answered that the mention of a number serves various purposes; here its purpose is extra emphasis, so it is beneficial despite not indicating negation. (2) Or the ruling is not accompanied by a number, like the example in the matn: 'Water from water' (hadith). ... The commentator is answering a presumed objection: Objection: How can your statement that specifying by a proper noun does not indicate the remaining others be correct, while this principle operates in fiqh narrations? Answer: The later scholars gave fatwa that this principle will operate in fiqh narrations but not in sacred texts. For example, the author of al-Hidayah defined a huge millstone as one whose movement on one side does not affect the other side, and then said, 'Wudu from the other side is permissible.' From this it is understood that wudu from the side where impurity fell is not permissible. ... The proof of the Hanafis: When the text indicates only that specific ruling, how can the unstated be proven? Because if the text is about affirmation, negation is unstated, and negation only covers the stated ruling, not the unstated. Clarification by example: The commentator says that if someone says, 'Zaid came,' this speech only indicates the coming of Zaid; as for whether another man came or not, it is silent on that. Similarly, 'Water from water' is a text on the obligation of ghusl from emission of mani, but it is silent on the obligation or non-obligation of ghusl from mere contact; that is known from another hadith: 'When the circumcised parts meet, ghusl becomes obligatory, whether or not one has ejaculated.' Similarly, 'Five from the harmful creatures...' does not indicate the ruling for other animals. ... The benefit of specification: Objection: When specifying by a proper noun is not beneficial for negating others, then what is its benefit?
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute about the principle of specification by a proper noun (تخصيص باسم العلم). The commentator is defending the Hanafi position that specifying something by a proper noun does not necessarily imply the negation of all other entities. The text begins with an argument from the Ansar Companions: if the specification had no benefit, they would not have used it to derive rulings. Then an objection is raised: if we accept this principle, the kalimah 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' would imply that no other prophet exists, which is false. The Hanafi response is that this principle does not apply here because the kalimah is about belief, not negation of other prophets; the objection itself is invalid. Next, two scenarios are discussed: (1) when the ruling is accompanied by a number, as in the hadith listing five harmful creatures. Some argue the number implies negation of others, but Hanafis say the number may be for emphasis, not negation. (2) When the ruling is without a number, like the hadith 'al-ma'u min al-ma'' (water from water), which is about ghusl from ejaculation. Then an objection is answered: how can this principle apply to fiqh narrations but not to sacred texts? The response is that later scholars (متأخرون) gave fatwa that the principle operates in fiqh narrations (like definitions of a millstone in al-Hidayah) but not in sacred texts like the Qur'an and authentic hadith. The Hanafi proof is that a text only indicates what it explicitly states; it is silent on other matters. Negation of others is not taken from explicit wording unless there is a clear indication. Examples: 'Zaid came' only indicates Zaid's coming, not negation of others; the ruling for ghusl from mere contact is derived from another hadith, not from 'al-ma'u min al-ma''. The passage ends with an objection about the benefit of specification if it does not negate others (the answer is cut off).

Key Terms
اسم العلم (ism al-alam): A proper noun that specifies a particular individual or thing.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification of a general ruling to a particular case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what does the hadith 'خمس من الفواسق' (five harmful creatures) indicate?
A) That only these five creatures are to be killed in ihram and outside it.
B) That these five creatures are to be killed, but the enumeration is for emphasis and does not negate killing other harmful creatures.
C) That no other creatures besides these five are harmful.
D) That the number five is the key point, and any other ruling is derived from this number.

Key Takeaway
Specifying a ruling by a proper noun or number does not automatically negate what is not mentioned; the Hanafi principle requires additional evidence to extend or restrict a ruling beyond what is explicitly stated.
